<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term <i>library</i> is based on the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">liber</i></span> for 'book' or 'document', contained in Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">libraria</i></span> 'collection of books' and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">librarium</i></span> 'container for books'. Other modern languages use derivations from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">βιβλιοθήκη</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">bibliothēkē</i></span>), originally meaning 'book container', via Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">bibliotheca</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Cf." title="Cf.">cf.</a> <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">bibliothèque</i></span> or <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Bibliothek</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_libraries" title="History of libraries">History of libraries</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Sistine_Hall_of_the_Vatican_Library_(2994335291).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/The_Sistine_Hall_of_the_Vatican_Library_%282994335291%29.jpg/220px-The_Sistine_Hall_of_the_Vatican_Library_%282994335291%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/The_Sistine_Hall_of_the_Vatican_Library_%282994335291%29.jpg/330px-The_Sistine_Hall_of_the_Vatican_Library_%282994335291%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/The_Sistine_Hall_of_the_Vatican_Library_%282994335291%29.jpg/440px-The_Sistine_Hall_of_the_Vatican_Library_%282994335291%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Sistine_Hall_of_the_Vatican_Library_(2994335291).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Sistine Hall of the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Library" title="Vatican Library">Vatican Library</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_libraries" title="History of libraries">history of libraries</a> began with the first efforts to organize collections of documents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-2">[2]</a></sup> The first libraries consisted of <a href="/wiki/Archive" title="Archive">archives</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Writing#The_beginning_of_writing" title="Writing">earliest form of writing</a>—the <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">clay tablets</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" title="Cuneiform script">cuneiform script</a> discovered in <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, some dating back to 2600 BC. Private or personal libraries made up of written books appeared in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">classical Greece</a> in the 5th century BC. In the 6th century, at the very close of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical period</a>, the great libraries of the Mediterranean world remained those of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Library_of_Constantinople" title="Imperial Library of Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Alexandria</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimids</a> (r. 909–1171) also possessed many great libraries within their domains. The historian <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Abi_Tayyi" title="Ibn Abi Tayyi">Ibn Abi Tayyi’</a> describes their palace library, which probably contained the largest collection of literature on earth at the time, as a "<a href="/wiki/Wonders_of_the_World" title="Wonders of the World">wonder of the world</a>". Throughout history, along with bloody massacres, the destruction of libraries has been critical for conquerors who wish to destroy every trace of the vanquished community's recorded memory. A prominent example of this can be found in the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_campaign_against_the_Nizaris" title="Mongol campaign against the Nizaris">Mongol massacre of the Nizaris</a> at Alamut in 1256 and the torching of their library, "the fame of which", boasts the conqueror Juwayni, "had spread throughout the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The libraries of <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a> were established in the fourteenth century and attracted scholars from all over the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions">Functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Libraries may provide physical or digital access to material, and may be a physical location or a virtual space, or both. A library's collection can include <a href="/wiki/Book" title="Book">books</a>, <a href="/wiki/Periodical_literature" title="Periodical literature">periodicals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">newspapers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscripts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">films</a>, <a href="/wiki/Map_collection" title="Map collection">maps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Print_room" title="Print room">prints</a>, <a href="/wiki/Document" title="Document">documents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microform" title="Microform">microform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">CDs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compact_Cassette" title="Compact Cassette">cassettes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Videotape" title="Videotape">videotapes</a>, <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVDs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray Discs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-book" title="E-book">e-books</a>, <a href="/wiki/Audiobook" title="Audiobook">audiobooks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">databases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tabletop_game" title="Tabletop game">table games</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video games</a> and other formats. Libraries range widely in size, up to millions of items.
</p><p>Libraries often provide quiet areas for studying, and they also often offer common areas to facilitate group study and collaboration. Libraries often provide public facilities for access to their electronic resources and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>. Public and institutional collections and services may be intended for use by people who choose not to—or cannot afford to—purchase an extensive collection themselves, who need material no individual can reasonably be expected to have, or who require professional assistance with their research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Services offered by a library are variously described as library services, information services, or the combination "library and information services", although different institutions and sources define such terminology differently. Organizations or departments are often called by one of these names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HK_Wan_Chai_Library_Inside_Bookcase_a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/HK_Wan_Chai_Library_Inside_Bookcase_a.jpg/220px-HK_Wan_Chai_Library_Inside_Bookcase_a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/HK_Wan_Chai_Library_Inside_Bookcase_a.jpg/330px-HK_Wan_Chai_Library_Inside_Bookcase_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/HK_Wan_Chai_Library_Inside_Bookcase_a.jpg/440px-HK_Wan_Chai_Library_Inside_Bookcase_a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HK_Wan_Chai_Library_Inside_Bookcase_a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Library shelves in Hong Kong, showing numbers of the classification scheme to help readers locate works in that section</div></div></div>
<p>Most libraries have materials arranged in a specified order according to a <a href="/wiki/Library_classification" title="Library classification">library classification</a> system, so that items may be located quickly and collections may be browsed efficiently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Some libraries have additional galleries beyond the public ones, where reference materials are stored. These reference stacks may be open to selected members of the public. Others require patrons to submit a "stack request", which is a request for an assistant to retrieve the material from the closed stacks: see <a href="/wiki/List_of_closed_stack_libraries" title="List of closed stack libraries">List of closed stack libraries</a>.
</p><p>Larger libraries are often divided into departments staffed by both paraprofessionals and professional librarians.
</p>
<ul><li>Circulation (or Access Services) – Handles user accounts and the loaning/returning and shelving of materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Collection_development" title="Collection development">Collection Development</a> – Orders materials and maintains materials budgets.</li>
<li>Reference – Staffs a <a href="/wiki/Reference_desk" title="Reference desk">reference desk</a> answering questions from users (using structured <a href="/wiki/Reference_interview" title="Reference interview">reference interviews</a>), instructing users, and developing library programming. Reference may be further broken down by user groups or materials; common collections are <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_literature" title="Children's literature">children's literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Young_adult_fiction" title="Young adult fiction">young adult literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a> materials.</li>
<li>Electronic Library - Responsible for providing information to users via electronic means</li>
<li>Technical Services – Works behind the scenes cataloging and processing new materials and deaccessioning <a href="/wiki/Weeding_(library)" title="Weeding (library)">weeded materials</a>.</li>
<li>Stacks Maintenance – Re-shelves materials that have been returned to the library after patron use and shelves materials that have been processed by Technical Services. Stacks Maintenance also shelf reads the material in the stacks to ensure that it is in the correct library classification order.</li></ul>
<p>Basic tasks in <a href="/wiki/Library_management" title="Library management">library management</a> include the planning of acquisitions (which materials the library should acquire, by purchase or otherwise), library classification of acquired materials, preservation of materials (especially rare and fragile archival materials such as manuscripts), the deaccessioning of materials, patron borrowing of materials, and developing and administering library computer systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> More long-term issues include the planning of the construction of new libraries or extensions to existing ones, and the development and implementation of outreach services and reading-enhancement services (such as adult literacy and children's programming). Library materials like books, magazines, periodicals, CDs, etc. are managed by Dewey Decimal Classification Theory and modified Dewey Decimal Classification Theory is more practical reliable system for library materials management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a> (ISO) has published several standards regarding the management of libraries through its Technical Committee 46 (TC 46),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> which is focused on "libraries, documentation and information centers, publishing, archives, records management, museum documentation, indexing and abstracting services, and information science". The following is a partial list of some of them:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>ISO 2789:2006 Information and documentation—International library statistics</li>
<li>ISO 11620:1998 Information and documentation—Library performance indicators</li>
<li>ISO 11799:2003 Information and documentation—Document storage requirements for archive and library materials</li>
<li>ISO 14416:2003 Information and documentation—Requirements for binding of books, periodicals, serials, and other paper documents for archive and library use—Methods and materials</li>
<li>ISO/TR 20983:2003 Information and documentation—Performance indicators for electronic library services</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage">Usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2009_3962573662_card_catalog.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/2009_3962573662_card_catalog.jpg/220px-2009_3962573662_card_catalog.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/2009_3962573662_card_catalog.jpg/330px-2009_3962573662_card_catalog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/2009_3962573662_card_catalog.jpg/440px-2009_3962573662_card_catalog.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2009_3962573662_card_catalog.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Until the advent of digital catalogues, <a href="/wiki/Library_catalog" title="Library catalog">card catalogues</a> were the traditional method of organizing the list of resources and their location within a large library.</div></div></div>
<p>Some patrons may not know how to fully use the library's resources. This can be due to individuals' unease in approaching a staff member. Ways in which a library's content is displayed or accessed may have the most impact on use. An antiquated or clumsy search system, or staff unwilling or untrained to engage their patrons, will limit a library's usefulness. In the <a href="/wiki/Public_library" title="Public library">public libraries</a> of the United States, beginning in the 19th century, these problems drove the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Library_instruction" title="Library instruction">library instruction</a> movement, which advocated library user education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> One of the early leaders was <a href="/wiki/John_Cotton_Dana" title="John Cotton Dana">John Cotton Dana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The basic form of library instruction is sometimes known as <a href="/wiki/Information_literacy" title="Information literacy">information literacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Libraries should inform their users of what materials are available in their collections and how to access that information. Before the computer age, this was accomplished by the card <a href="/wiki/Library_catalog" title="Library catalog">catalogue</a>—a cabinet (or multiple cabinets) containing many drawers filled with <a href="/wiki/Index_card" title="Index card">index cards</a> that identified books and other materials. In a large library, the card catalogue often filled a large room.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>, however, has led to the adoption of electronic catalogue databases (often referred to as "webcats" or as <a href="/wiki/Online_public_access_catalog" title="Online public access catalog">online public access catalogues</a>, OPACs), which allow users to search the library's holdings from any location with Internet access.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This style of catalogue maintenance is compatible with new types of libraries, such as <a href="/wiki/Digital_library" title="Digital library">digital libraries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_library" title="Distributed library">distributed libraries</a>, as well as older libraries that have been <a href="/wiki/Retrofitting" title="Retrofitting">retrofitted</a>. Large libraries may be scattered within multiple buildings across a town, each having multiple floors, with multiple rooms housing their resources across a series of shelves called <a href="/wiki/Bay_(shelving)" title="Bay (shelving)">bays</a>. Once a user has located a resource within the catalogue, they must then use navigational guidance to retrieve the resource physically, a process that may be assisted through signage, maps, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS" title="GPS">GPS</a> systems, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RFID" title="RFID">RFID</a> tagging.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> has the highest number of registered book borrowers per capita in the world. Over half of Finland's population are registered borrowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In the US, public library users have borrowed on average roughly 15 books per user per year from 1856 to 1978. From 1978 to 2004, book circulation per user declined approximately 50%. The growth of audiovisuals circulation, estimated at 25% of total circulation in 2004, accounts for about half of this decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_the_Internet">Relationship with the Internet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Relationship with the Internet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Museum_Reading_Room_Panorama_Feb_2006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1701" data-file-width="4996" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/British_Museum_Reading_Room_Panorama_Feb_2006.jpg/300px-British_Museum_Reading_Room_Panorama_Feb_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/British_Museum_Reading_Room_Panorama_Feb_2006.jpg/450px-British_Museum_Reading_Room_Panorama_Feb_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/British_Museum_Reading_Room_Panorama_Feb_2006.jpg/600px-British_Museum_Reading_Room_Panorama_Feb_2006.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Museum_Reading_Room_Panorama_Feb_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/British_Museum_Reading_Room" title="British Museum Reading Room">British Museum Reading Room</a></div></div></div>
<p>A library may make use of the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> in a number of ways, from creating their own <a href="/wiki/Library_website" title="Library website">library website</a> to making the contents of its <a href="/wiki/Online_public_access_catalog" title="Online public access catalog">catalogues searchable online</a>. Some specialised search engines such as <a href="/wiki/Google_Scholar" title="Google Scholar">Google Scholar</a> offer a way to facilitate searching for academic resources such as journal articles and research papers. The <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">Online Computer Library Center</a> allows anyone to search the world's largest repository of library records through its <a href="/wiki/WorldCat" title="WorldCat">WorldCat</a> online database.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grossman(2009)_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grossman(2009)-25">[25]</a></sup> Websites such as <a href="/wiki/LibraryThing" title="LibraryThing">LibraryThing</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a> provide abstracts, reviews, and recommendations of books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grossman(2009)_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grossman(2009)-25">[25]</a></sup> Libraries provide computers and Internet access to allow people to search for information online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Online information access is particularly attractive to younger library users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA,_DCMS,_LASER(2006)_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLA,_DCMS,_LASER(2006)-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digitizing" title="Digitizing">Digitization</a> of books, particularly those that are <a href="/wiki/Out_of_print" title="Out of print">out-of-print</a>, in projects such as <a href="/wiki/Google_Books" title="Google Books">Google Books</a> provides resources for library and other online users. Due to their holdings of valuable material, some libraries are important partners for search engines such as <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> in realizing the potential of such projects and have received reciprocal benefits in cases where they have negotiated effectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Darnton(2009)_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Darnton(2009)-32">[32]</a></sup> As the prominence of and reliance on the Internet has grown, library services have moved the emphasis from mainly providing print resources to providing more computers and more <a href="/wiki/Internet_access" title="Internet access">Internet access</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Libraries face a number of challenges in adapting to new ways of <a href="/wiki/Information_seeking" title="Information seeking">information seeking</a> that may stress convenience over quality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> reducing the priority of <a href="/wiki/Information_literacy" title="Information literacy">information literacy</a> skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell(2005)_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bell(2005)-35">[35]</a></sup> The potential decline in library usage, particularly <a href="/wiki/Reference_desk" title="Reference desk">reference services</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> puts the necessity for these services in doubt.
</p><p>Library scholars have acknowledged that libraries need to address the ways that they market their services if they are to compete with the Internet and mitigate the risk of losing users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> This includes promoting the information literacy skills training considered vital across the library profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell(2005)_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bell(2005)-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CILIP(2010)_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-CILIP(2010)-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenney(2004)_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenney(2004)-39">[39]</a></sup> Many US based research librarians rely on the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_College_and_Research_Libraries" title="Association of College and Research Libraries">ACRL</a> Framework for Information Literacy in order to guide students and faculty in research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> However, marketing of services has to be adequately supported financially in order to be successful. This can be problematic for library services that are publicly funded and find it difficult to justify diverting tight funds to apparently peripheral areas such as branding and marketing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Privacy" title="Privacy">privacy</a> aspect of library usage in the Internet age is a matter of growing concern and advocacy; privacy workshops are run by the <a href="/wiki/Library_Freedom_Project" title="Library Freedom Project">Library Freedom Project</a> which teach librarians about digital tools (such as the <a href="/wiki/Tor_(network)" title="Tor (network)">Tor network</a>) to thwart mass surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Librarians">Librarians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Librarians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Education_for_librarianship" title="Education for librarianship">Education for librarianship</a></div>
<p>Libraries are usually staffed by a combination of professionally-trained librarians, paraprofessional staff sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Library_technician" title="Library technician">library technicians</a>, and support staff. Some topics related to the <a href="/wiki/Education_for_librarianship" title="Education for librarianship">education of librarians</a> and allied staff include accessibility of the collection, acquisition of materials, arrangement and finding tools, the book trade, the influence of the physical properties of the different writing materials, language distribution, role in education, rates of literacy, budgets, staffing, libraries for specially targeted audiences, architectural merit, patterns of usage, the role of libraries in a nation's cultural heritage, and the role of government, church or private sponsorship. Since the 1960s, issues of computerization and digitization have arisen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many institutions make a distinction between a circulating or <a href="/wiki/Lending_library" title="Lending library">lending library</a>, where materials are expected and intended to be loaned to patrons, institutions, or other libraries, and a reference library where material is not lent out. Travelling libraries, such as the early horseback libraries of eastern Kentucky<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bookmobiles" title="Bookmobiles">bookmobiles</a>, are generally of the lending type. Modern libraries are often a mixture of both, containing a general collection for circulation, and a reference collection which is restricted to the library premises. Also, increasingly, digital collections enable broader access to material that may not circulate in print, and enables libraries to expand their collections even without building a larger facility. Lamba (2019) reinforced this idea by observing that "today's libraries have become increasingly multi-disciplinary, collaborative and networked" and that applying <a href="/wiki/Web_2.0" title="Web 2.0">Web 2.0</a> tools to libraries would "not only connect the users with their community and enhance communication but will also help the librarians to promote their library's activities, services, and products to target both their actual and potential users".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_libraries">Academic libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Academic libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Academic_library" title="Academic library">Academic library</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egyetemi_K%C3%B6nyvt%C3%A1r4.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2432" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Egyetemi_K%C3%B6nyvt%C3%A1r4.JPG/220px-Egyetemi_K%C3%B6nyvt%C3%A1r4.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Egyetemi_K%C3%B6nyvt%C3%A1r4.JPG/330px-Egyetemi_K%C3%B6nyvt%C3%A1r4.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Egyetemi_K%C3%B6nyvt%C3%A1r4.JPG/440px-Egyetemi_K%C3%B6nyvt%C3%A1r4.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egyetemi_K%C3%B6nyvt%C3%A1r4.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The University Library at <a href="/wiki/E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s_Lor%C3%A1nd_University" title="Eötvös Loránd University">Eötvös Loránd University</a>, Budapest, Hungary</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_Libraries" title="Academic Libraries">Academic libraries</a> are generally located on college and university campuses and primarily serve the students and faculty of that and other academic institutions. Some academic libraries, especially those at public institutions, are accessible to members of the general public in whole or in part. Library services are sometimes extended to the general public at a fee, some academic libraries create such services in order to enhance literacy levels in their communities.
</p><p>Academic libraries are libraries that are hosted in post-secondary educational institutions, such as colleges and universities. Their main functions are to provide support in research, consultancy and resource linkage for students and faculty of the educational institution. Academic libraries house current, reliable and relevant information resources spread through all the disciplines which serve to assuage the information requirements of students and faculty. In cases where not all books are housed some libraries have E-resources, where they subscribe for a given institution they are serving, in order to provide backups and additional information that is not practical to have available as hard copies. Furthermore, most libraries <a href="/wiki/Interlibrary_loan" title="Interlibrary loan">collaborate with other libraries</a> in exchange of books.
</p><p>Specific course-related resources are usually provided by the library, such as copies of textbooks and article readings held on 'reserve' (meaning that they are loaned out only on a short-term basis, usually a matter of hours). Some academic libraries provide resources not usually associated with libraries, such as the ability to check out laptop computers, web cameras, or scientific calculators.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Robartslibrary.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Robartslibrary.jpg/220px-Robartslibrary.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Robartslibrary.jpg/330px-Robartslibrary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Robartslibrary.jpg/440px-Robartslibrary.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Robartslibrary.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Robarts_Library" title="Robarts Library">Robarts Library</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Toronto" title="University of Toronto">University of Toronto</a>, Canada</div></div></div><p>Academic libraries offer workshops and courses outside of formal, graded coursework, which are meant to provide students with the tools necessary to succeed in their programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> These workshops may include help with citations, effective search techniques, journal databases, and electronic citation software. These workshops provide students with skills that can help them achieve success in their academic careers (and often, in their future occupations), which they may not learn inside the classroom.
</p><p>The academic library provides a quiet study space for students on campus; it may also provide group study space, such as meeting rooms. In North America, Europe, and other parts of the world, academic libraries are becoming increasingly digitally oriented. The library provides a "gateway" for students and researchers to access various resources, both print/physical and digital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Academic institutions are subscribing to electronic journals databases, providing research and scholarly writing software, and usually provide computer workstations or computer labs for students to access journals, library search databases and portals, institutional electronic resources, Internet access, and course- or task-related software (i.e. word processing and spreadsheet software). Some academic libraries take on new roles, for instance, acting as an electronic repository for institutional scholarly research and academic knowledge, such as the collection and curation of digital copies of students' theses and dissertations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Moreover, academic libraries are increasingly acting as publishers on their own on a not-for-profit basis, especially in the form of fully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_Access" title="Open Access">Open Access</a> institutional publishers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Children.27s_libraries"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Children's_libraries">Children's libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Children's libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Children's library" redirects here. For the collection of digitized books, see <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive%27s_Children%27s_Library" title="Internet Archive's Children's Library">Internet Archive's Children's Library</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Children._Children%27s_Library_in_N.D.G_BAnQ_Vieux-Montr%C3%A9al_P48S1P08742.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Children._Children%27s_Library_in_N.D.G_BAnQ_Vieux-Montr%C3%A9al_P48S1P08742.jpg/220px-Children._Children%27s_Library_in_N.D.G_BAnQ_Vieux-Montr%C3%A9al_P48S1P08742.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Children._Children%27s_Library_in_N.D.G_BAnQ_Vieux-Montr%C3%A9al_P48S1P08742.jpg/330px-Children._Children%27s_Library_in_N.D.G_BAnQ_Vieux-Montr%C3%A9al_P48S1P08742.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Children._Children%27s_Library_in_N.D.G_BAnQ_Vieux-Montr%C3%A9al_P48S1P08742.jpg/440px-Children._Children%27s_Library_in_N.D.G_BAnQ_Vieux-Montr%C3%A9al_P48S1P08742.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Children._Children%27s_Library_in_N.D.G_BAnQ_Vieux-Montr%C3%A9al_P48S1P08742.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A children's library in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, Quebec, Canada in 1943</div></div></div>
<p>Children's libraries are special collections of books intended for juvenile readers and usually kept in separate rooms of general public libraries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Some children's libraries have entire floors or wings dedicated to them in bigger libraries while smaller ones may have a separate room or area for children. They are an educational agency seeking to acquaint the young with the world's literature and to cultivate a love for reading. Their work supplements that of the public schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Services commonly provided by public libraries may include storytelling sessions for infants, toddlers, preschool children, or after-school programs, all with an intention of developing early literacy skills and a love of books. One of the most popular programs offered in public libraries are summer reading programs for children, families, and adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another popular reading program for children is PAWS TO READ or similar programs where children can read to certified therapy dogs. Since animals are a calming influence and there is no judgment, children learn confidence and a love of reading. Many states have these types of programs: parents need simply ask their librarian to see if it is available at their local library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_libraries">National libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: National libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_library" title="National library">National library</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_Library_of_Wales.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/National_Library_of_Wales.jpg/220px-National_Library_of_Wales.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/National_Library_of_Wales.jpg/330px-National_Library_of_Wales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/National_Library_of_Wales.jpg/440px-National_Library_of_Wales.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_Library_of_Wales.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Wales" title="National Library of Wales">National Library of Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aberystwyth" title="Aberystwyth">Aberystwyth</a></div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/National_library" title="National library">national or state library</a> serves as a national repository of information, and has the right of <a href="/wiki/Legal_deposit" title="Legal deposit">legal deposit</a>, which is a legal requirement that publishers in the country need to deposit a copy of each publication with the library. Unlike a public library, a national library rarely allows citizens to borrow books. Often, their collections include numerous rare, valuable, or significant works. There are wider definitions of a national library, putting less emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Institutional_repository" title="Institutional repository">repository</a> character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The first national libraries had their origins in the royal collections of the sovereign or some other supreme body of the state.
</p><p>Many national libraries cooperate within the National Libraries Section of the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Library_Associations_and_Institutions" title="International Federation of Library Associations and Institutions">International Federation of Library Associations and Institutions</a> (IFLA) to discuss their common tasks, define and promote common standards, and carry out projects helping them to fulfill their duties. The national libraries of Europe participate in <a href="/wiki/European_Library" title="European Library">The European Library</a> which is a service of the Conference of European National Librarians (CENL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_lending_libraries">Public lending libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Public lending libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Public_library" title="Public library">Public library</a></div><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ET_Amhara_asv2018-02_img120_Awra_Amba.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5304" data-file-width="7952" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/ET_Amhara_asv2018-02_img120_Awra_Amba.jpg/220px-ET_Amhara_asv2018-02_img120_Awra_Amba.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/ET_Amhara_asv2018-02_img120_Awra_Amba.jpg/330px-ET_Amhara_asv2018-02_img120_Awra_Amba.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/ET_Amhara_asv2018-02_img120_Awra_Amba.jpg/440px-ET_Amhara_asv2018-02_img120_Awra_Amba.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ET_Amhara_asv2018-02_img120_Awra_Amba.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A community library in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a></div></div></div>
<p>A public library provides services to the general public. If the library is part of a countywide library system, citizens with an active library card from around that county can use the library branches associated with the library system. A library can serve only their city, however, if they are not a member of the county public library system. Much of the materials located within a public library are available for borrowing. The library staff decides upon the number of items patrons are allowed to borrow, as well as the details of borrowing time allotted. Typically, libraries issue <a href="/wiki/Library_card" title="Library card">library cards</a> to community members wishing to borrow books. Often visitors to a city are able to obtain a public library card.
</p><p>Many public libraries also serve as community organizations that provide free services and events to the public, such as reading groups and toddler story time. For many communities, the library is a source of connection to a vast world, obtainable knowledge and understanding, and entertainment. According to a study by the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Library_Association" title="Pennsylvania Library Association">Pennsylvania Library Association</a>, public library services play a major role in fighting rising illiteracy rates among youths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Public libraries are protected and funded by the public they serve.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bates_Hall_-_Boston_Public_Library.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bates_Hall_-_Boston_Public_Library.jpg/220px-Bates_Hall_-_Boston_Public_Library.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bates_Hall_-_Boston_Public_Library.jpg/330px-Bates_Hall_-_Boston_Public_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bates_Hall_-_Boston_Public_Library.jpg/440px-Bates_Hall_-_Boston_Public_Library.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bates_Hall_-_Boston_Public_Library.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bates Hall, the main reading room of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Public_Library" title="Boston Public Library">Boston Public Library, Boston, Massachusetts, United States</a></div></div></div>
<p>As the number of books in libraries have steadily increased since their inception, the need for compact storage and access with adequate lighting has grown. The <i><a href="/wiki/Library_stack" title="Library stack">stack system</a></i> involves keeping a library's collection of books in a space separate from the reading room. This arrangement arose in the 19th century. Book stacks quickly evolved into a fairly standard form in which the cast iron and steel frameworks supporting the bookshelves also supported the floors, which often were built of translucent blocks to permit the passage of light (but were not transparent, for reasons of modesty). The introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_lights" title="Electric lights">electric lights</a> had a huge impact on <a href="/wiki/Lighting_in_libraries" title="Lighting in libraries">lighting in libraries</a>. The use of glass floors was largely discontinued, though floors were still often composed of metal grating to allow air to circulate in multi-story stacks. As more space was needed, a method of moving shelves on tracks (compact shelving) was introduced to cut down on otherwise wasted aisle space.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Library_2.0" title="Library 2.0">Library 2.0</a>, a term coined in 2005, is the library's response to the challenge of Google and an attempt to meet the changing needs of users by using <a href="/wiki/Web_2.0" title="Web 2.0">Web 2.0</a> technology. Some of the aspects of Library 2.0 include, commenting, tagging, bookmarking, discussions, use of online social networks by libraries, <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_(computing)" title="Plug-in (computing)">plug-ins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Software_widget" title="Software widget">widgets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Inspired by Web 2.0, it is an attempt to make the library a more user-driven institution.
</p><p>Despite the importance ascribed to public libraries, their budgets are often cut by legislatures. In some cases, funding has dwindled so much that libraries have been forced to cut their hours and release employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reference_libraries">Reference libraries<span class="anchor" id="Reference_library"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reference libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Main_Reading_Room_of_the_New_York_City_Public_Library_on_5th_Avenue_ca,_1910-1920.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="691" data-file-width="886" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Main_Reading_Room_of_the_New_York_City_Public_Library_on_5th_Avenue_ca%2C_1910-1920.jpg/220px-Main_Reading_Room_of_the_New_York_City_Public_Library_on_5th_Avenue_ca%2C_1910-1920.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Main_Reading_Room_of_the_New_York_City_Public_Library_on_5th_Avenue_ca%2C_1910-1920.jpg/330px-Main_Reading_Room_of_the_New_York_City_Public_Library_on_5th_Avenue_ca%2C_1910-1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Main_Reading_Room_of_the_New_York_City_Public_Library_on_5th_Avenue_ca%2C_1910-1920.jpg/440px-Main_Reading_Room_of_the_New_York_City_Public_Library_on_5th_Avenue_ca%2C_1910-1920.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Main_Reading_Room_of_the_New_York_City_Public_Library_on_5th_Avenue_ca,_1910-1920.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main reading room of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_City_Public_Library" title="New York City Public Library">New York City Public Library</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5th_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="5th Avenue (Manhattan)">5th Avenue</a> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1910–1920</span></div></div></div>
<p>A reference library does not lend books and other items; instead, they can only be read at the library itself. Typically, such libraries are used for research purposes, for example at a university. Some items at reference libraries may be historical and even unique. Many lending libraries contain a "reference section", which holds books, such as dictionaries, which are common reference books, and are therefore not lent out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Such reference sections may be referred to as "reading rooms", which may also include newspapers and periodicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> An example of a reading room is the Hazel H. Ransom Reading Room at the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Ransom_Center" title="Harry Ransom Center">Harry Ransom Center</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a>, which maintains the papers of literary agent <a href="/wiki/Audrey_Wood_(literary_agent)" title="Audrey Wood (literary agent)">Audrey Wood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ransom_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ransom-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_libraries">Research libraries<span class="anchor" id="Research_library"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Research libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Research_library" title="Research library">Research library</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quaid-e-Azam_Library_in_Jinnah_Garden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Quaid-e-Azam_Library_in_Jinnah_Garden.jpg/220px-Quaid-e-Azam_Library_in_Jinnah_Garden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Quaid-e-Azam_Library_in_Jinnah_Garden.jpg/330px-Quaid-e-Azam_Library_in_Jinnah_Garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Quaid-e-Azam_Library_in_Jinnah_Garden.jpg/440px-Quaid-e-Azam_Library_in_Jinnah_Garden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quaid-e-Azam_Library_in_Jinnah_Garden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Quaid-e-Azam_Library" title="Quaid-e-Azam Library">Quaid-e-Azam Library</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bagh-e-Jinnah,_Lahore" title="Bagh-e-Jinnah, Lahore">Bagh-e-Jinnah</a>, Lahore, Pakistan</div></div></div>
<p>A research library is a collection of materials on one or more subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> A research library supports scholarly or scientific research and will generally include <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">primary</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Secondary_source" title="Secondary source">secondary sources</a>; it will maintain permanent collections and attempt to provide access to all necessary materials. A research library is most often an <a href="/wiki/Academic_library" title="Academic library">academic</a> or <a href="/wiki/National_library" title="National library">national library</a>, but a large <a href="/wiki/Special_library" title="Special library">special library</a> may have a research library within its special field, and a very few of the largest public libraries also serve as research libraries. A large university library may be considered a research library; and in North America, such libraries may belong to the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Research_Libraries" title="Association of Research Libraries">Association of Research Libraries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In the United Kingdom, they may be members of <a href="/wiki/Research_Libraries_UK" title="Research Libraries UK">Research Libraries UK (RLUK)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>A research library can be either a reference library, which does not lend its holdings, or a lending library, which does lend all or some of its holdings. Some extremely large or traditional research libraries are entirely reference in this sense, lending none of their materials; most academic research libraries, at least in the US and the UK, now lend books, but not periodicals or other materials. Many research libraries are attached to a parental organization and serve only members of that organization. Examples of research libraries include the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a> at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Public_Library_Main_Branch" title="New York Public Library Main Branch">New York Public Library Main Branch</a> on 42nd Street in Manhattan, <a href="/wiki/State_Public_Scientific_%26_Technological_Library" title="State Public Scientific &amp; Technological Library">State Public Scientific Technological Library of the Siberian Branch of the Russian Academy of Science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_libraries">Digital libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Digital libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Digital_library" title="Digital library">Digital library</a></div>
<p>Digital libraries are libraries that house digital resources. They are defined as an organization and not a service that provide access to digital works, have a preservation responsibility to provide future access to materials, and provides these items easily and affordably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chowdhury_&amp;_Foo_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chowdhury_&amp;_Foo-71">[71]</a></sup> The definition of a digital library implies that "a digital library uses a variety of software, networking technologies and standards to facilitate access to digital content and data to a designated user community."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChowdhuryFoo20123–4_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChowdhuryFoo20123–4-72">[72]</a></sup> Access to digital libraries can be influenced by several factors, either individually or together. The most common factors that influence access are: The library's content, the characteristics and information needs of the target users, the library's digital interface, the goals and objectives of the library's organizational structure, and the standards and regulations that govern library use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChowdhuryFoo201249_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChowdhuryFoo201249-73">[73]</a></sup> Access will depend on the users ability to discover and retrieve documents that interest them and that they require, which in turn is a preservation question. Digital objects cannot be preserved passively, they must be curated by digital librarians to ensure the trust and integrity of the digital objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the biggest considerations for digital librarians is the need to provide long-term access to their resources; to do this, there are two issues requiring watchfulness: Media failure and format obsolescence. With media failure, a particular digital item is unusable because of some sort of error or problem. A scratched CD-ROM, for example, will not display its contents correctly, but another, unscratched disk will not have that problem. Format obsolescence is when a digital format has been superseded by newer technology, and so items in the old format are unreadable and unusable. Dealing with media failure is a reactive process, because something is done only when a problem presents itself. In contrast, format obsolescence is preparatory, because changes are anticipated and solutions are sought before there is a problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChowdhuryFoo2012211_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChowdhuryFoo2012211-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Future trends in digital preservation include: Transparent enterprise models for digital preservation, launch of self-preserving objects, increased flexibility in digital preservation architectures, clearly defined metrics for comparing preservation tools, and terminology and standards interoperability in real time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChowdhuryFoo2012211_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChowdhuryFoo2012211-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_libraries">Special libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Special libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Special_library" title="Special library">Special library</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bookshelf_at_Yale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4912" data-file-width="7360" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bookshelf_at_Yale.jpg/220px-Bookshelf_at_Yale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bookshelf_at_Yale.jpg/330px-Bookshelf_at_Yale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bookshelf_at_Yale.jpg/440px-Bookshelf_at_Yale.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bookshelf_at_Yale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bookshelf at the <a href="/wiki/Beinecke_Rare_Book_%26_Manuscript_Library" title="Beinecke Rare Book &amp; Manuscript Library">Beinecke Rare Book &amp; Manuscript Library</a>. The top floor contains 180,000 volumes. Since 1977, all new acquisitions are frozen at −33 °F (−36 °C) to prevent the spread of insects and diseases.</div></div></div>
<p>Many private businesses and public organizations, including hospitals, churches, museums, research laboratories, law firms, and many government departments and agencies, maintain their own libraries for the use of their employees in doing specialized research related to their work. Depending on the particular institution, special libraries may or may not be accessible to the general public or elements thereof. In more specialized institutions such as law firms and research laboratories, librarians employed in special libraries are commonly specialists in the institution's field rather than generally trained librarians, and often are not required to have advanced degrees in a specifically library-related field due to the specialized content and clientele of the library.
</p><p>Special libraries can also include women's libraries or LGBTQ libraries, which serve the needs of women and the LGBTQ community. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libraries_and_the_LGBTQ_community" title="Libraries and the LGBTQ community">Libraries and the LGBTQ community</a> have an extensive history, and there are currently many libraries, archives, and special collections devoted to preserving and helping the LGBTQ community. Women's libraries, such as the Vancouver Women's Library or the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Library" title="Women's Library">Women's Library @LSE</a> are examples of women's libraries that offer services to women and girls and focus on women's history.
</p><p>Some special libraries, such as governmental law libraries, hospital libraries, and military base libraries commonly are open to public visitors to the institution in question. Depending on the particular library and the clientele it serves, special libraries may offer services similar to research, reference, public, academic, or children's libraries, often with restrictions such as only lending books to patients at a hospital or restricting the public from parts of a military collection. Given the highly individual nature of special libraries, visitors to a special library are often advised to check what services and restrictions apply at that particular library.
</p><p>Special libraries are distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Special_collections" title="Special collections">special collections</a>, which are branches or parts of a library intended for rare books, manuscripts, and other special materials, though some special libraries have special collections of their own, typically related to the library's specialized subject area.
</p><p>For more information on specific types of special libraries, see <a href="/wiki/Law_library" title="Law library">law libraries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medical_library" title="Medical library">medical libraries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_library" title="Music library">music libraries</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Transportation_libraries" title="Transportation libraries">transportation libraries</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_libraries_2">Digital libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Digital libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Digital_library" title="Digital library">Digital library</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bibliotheca_Alexandrina_interior_-_2008-07-17.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1872" data-file-width="3328" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bibliotheca_Alexandrina_interior_-_2008-07-17.JPG/220px-Bibliotheca_Alexandrina_interior_-_2008-07-17.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bibliotheca_Alexandrina_interior_-_2008-07-17.JPG/330px-Bibliotheca_Alexandrina_interior_-_2008-07-17.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bibliotheca_Alexandrina_interior_-_2008-07-17.JPG/440px-Bibliotheca_Alexandrina_interior_-_2008-07-17.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bibliotheca_Alexandrina_interior_-_2008-07-17.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior of the <a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_Alexandrina" title="Bibliotheca Alexandrina">Bibliotheca Alexandrina</a>, Alexandria, Egypt, showing both stacks and computer terminals</div></div></div>
<p>In the 21st century, there has been increasing use of the Internet to gather and retrieve data. The shift to digital libraries has greatly impacted the way people use physical libraries. Between 2002 and 2004, the average American academic library saw the overall number of transactions decline approximately 2.2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Libraries are trying to keep up with the digital world and the new generation of students that are used to having information just one click away. For example, the University of California Library System saw a 54% decline in circulation between 1991 and 2001 of 8,377,000 books to 3,832,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>These facts might be a consequence of the increased availability of e-resources. In 1999–2000, 105 ARL university libraries spent almost $100 million on electronic resources, which is an increase of nearly $23 million from the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> A 2003 report by the Open E-book Forum found that close to a million e-books had been sold in 2002, generating nearly $8 million in revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Another example of the shift to digital libraries can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Cushing_Academy" title="Cushing Academy">Cushing Academy</a>'s decision to dispense with its library of printed books—more than 20,000 volumes in all—and switch over entirely to digital media resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vista_de_la_Biblioteca_Vasconcelos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="957" data-file-width="1278" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Vista_de_la_Biblioteca_Vasconcelos.jpg/220px-Vista_de_la_Biblioteca_Vasconcelos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Vista_de_la_Biblioteca_Vasconcelos.jpg/330px-Vista_de_la_Biblioteca_Vasconcelos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Vista_de_la_Biblioteca_Vasconcelos.jpg/440px-Vista_de_la_Biblioteca_Vasconcelos.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vista_de_la_Biblioteca_Vasconcelos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stacks of the <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Vasconcelos" title="Biblioteca Vasconcelos">José Vasconcelos Library</a> in Mexico City, Mexico</div></div></div>
<p>A 2001 discussion paper exploring changing use of libraries reported decreased library usage by undergraduate students, who had become more used to retrieving information from the Internet than a traditional library. Finding information by simply searching the Internet is seen as easier and faster than reading an entire book. In a survey conducted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NetLibrary" title="NetLibrary">NetLibrary</a>, 93% of undergraduate students said that finding information online made more sense to them than going to the library. Three-quarters said they did not have enough time to go to the library. While the retrieving information from the Internet may be efficient and time-saving than visiting a traditional library, research showed that undergraduates are most likely searching only .03% of the entire web.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-2000s, Swedish company Distec invented a library book vending machine known as the <a href="/wiki/GoLibrary" title="GoLibrary">GoLibrary</a>, that offers library books to people where there is no branch, limited hours, or high traffic locations such as <a href="/wiki/El_Cerrito_del_Norte_station" title="El Cerrito del Norte station">El Cerrito del Norte</a> <a href="/wiki/Bay_Area_Rapid_Transit" title="Bay Area Rapid Transit">BART station</a> in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Associations">Associations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Associations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_library_associations" title="List of library associations">List of library associations</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Library_Associations_and_Institutions" title="International Federation of Library Associations and Institutions">International Federation of Library Associations and Institutions</a> (IFLA) is the leading international association of library organizations. It is the global voice of the library and information profession, and its annual conference provides a venue for librarians to learn from one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Library associations in Asia include the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Library_Association" title="Indian Library Association">Indian Library Association</a> (ILA),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Indian Association of Special Libraries and Information Centers (IASLIC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Bengal Library Association (BLA), Kolkata,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Library_Association" title="Pakistan Library Association">Pakistan Library Association</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> the Pakistan Librarians Welfare Organization,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Association_of_Librarians,_Information_Scientists_and_Documentalists" title="Bangladesh Association of Librarians, Information Scientists and Documentalists">Bangladesh Association of Librarians, Information Scientists and Documentalists</a>, the Library Association of Bangladesh, and the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_Library_Association" title="Sri Lanka Library Association">Sri Lanka Library Association</a> (founded 1960).
</p><p>National associations of the English-speaking world include the <a href="/wiki/American_Library_Association" title="American Library Association">American Library Association</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Library_and_Information_Association" title="Australian Library and Information Association">Australian Library and Information Association</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Library_Association" title="Canadian Library Association">Canadian Library Association</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LIANZA" title="LIANZA">Library and Information Association of New Zealand Aotearoa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Research_Libraries_UK" title="Research Libraries UK">Research Libraries UK</a> (a consortium of 30 university and other research libraries in the United Kingdom). Library bodies such as <a href="/wiki/Chartered_Institute_of_Library_and_Information_Professionals" title="Chartered Institute of Library and Information Professionals">CILIP</a> (formerly the Library Association, founded 1877) may advocate the role that libraries and librarians can play in a modern Internet environment, and in the teaching of <a href="/wiki/Information_literacy" title="Information literacy">information literacy</a> skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CILIP(2010)_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-CILIP(2010)-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowlandsetal.(2008)_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rowlandsetal.(2008)-90">[90]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Library_Association" title="Nigerian Library Association">Nigerian Library Association</a> is the recognized group for librarians working in Nigeria. It was established in 1962 in Ibadan.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Public_library_advocacy" title="Public library advocacy">Public library advocacy</a> is support given to a public library for its financial and philosophical goals or needs. Most often this takes the form of monetary or material donations or campaigning to the institutions which oversee the library, sometimes by advocacy groups such as <a href="/wiki/Friends_of_Libraries" title="Friends of Libraries">Friends of Libraries</a> and community members. Originally, library advocacy was centered on the library itself, but current trends show libraries positioning themselves to demonstrate they provide "economic value to the community" in means that are not directly related to the checking out of books and other media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Protection">Protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Libraries are considered part of the cultural heritage and are one of the primary objectives in many state and domestic conflicts and are at risk of destruction and looting. Financing is often carried out by robbing valuable library items. National and international coordination regarding military and civil structures for the protection of libraries is operated by <a href="/wiki/Blue_Shield_International" title="Blue Shield International">Blue Shield International</a> and <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>. From an international perspective, despite the partial dissolution of state structures and very unclear security situations as a result of the wars and unrest, robust undertakings to protect libraries are being carried out. The topic is also the creation of "no-strike lists", in which the coordinates of important cultural monuments such as libraries have been preserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
